Venous phlebotomy
The veins used for phlebotomy are: 30, of which 16 are in the head. There are two pulsating veins near the ears called "auriculars," two herbs on both temples, two veins in the lacrimal ducts of both eyes called "aspectantes," one vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and another at the tip of the nose; two jugular veins at the neck; two veins above the upper lip; two veins inside the lower lip; two veins behind the tongue called "algeletic"; and two veins on either side of the tongue. The veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five:
"And in the foot and leg there are three veins: one of these is that which leaves the curvature of the wild boar's tooth; the second is saphenous, and its location is near the leg of the domestic dog; and the sciatic vein, and its location is near the leg of the wild boar; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those."
"The benefit of treating veins near the ears is for old-fashioned headaches and migraines, and for healing wounds and ulcers on the head from ancient times."
"And the method of letting blood from these veins is as follows: it should be done on a clear day, and the patient's head should be shaved in the occiput area where both veins are located, using a wide cloth with good friction; then strongly bind the patient's head with a bandage that appears at both locations near the ears in two points pressed down on the head; then seek that vein with your finger, and if you feel its pulse with your finger, here signal to stop."
"Then take the lancet knife, which is that which is inserted, and carefully insert it into the vein in the elbow until the bleeding comes to the bone." "And then raise your hand with the vein and elbow upwards, and incise the vein with an indirect incision." "The length of the incision should be equal to two fingers placed side by side or nearby; let blood flow out as much as desired."
"And sprinkle powder on it and leave that until healed."
"And as already mentioned at the beginning of this book, an incision is made there and they are cauterized."
"More about the two great veins that are in the temples: the benefit of phlebotomy is for ancient migraine, persistent headache, and frequent eye inflammation, as well as acute excess blood flowing to the heart."
"And the quality of performing phlebotomy on them is, as I recommend to you: compress the illness with your hand until the two veins appear clearly through touch and sensation; then, feel their pulse with your fingertips, and at that moment strike [with the lancet]."

"And then release the compression of the illness, place your finger over the vein for an hour to stop the bleeding, and apply pressure on that spot with cotton and powder, pressing it down firmly. Leave it until healed."
"And this has already been preceded by the memory of them [the two great veins], their incision, and attraction at the beginning of this book."

"And the veins that are commonly phlebotomized in the elbow are those which are present in all humans. "Of the remedies for phlebotomy of the basilica vein, which is one of three veins: for it draws out the blood from illnesses that are below the throat and the stomach, and thus relieves the chest and abdomen."

"And if blood flows during phlebotomy like a child's urine, and it is thin and red, know then that it is arterial blood. "And therefore if the blood stops flowing, that is good. And if not, then treat it with whatever has been previously mentioned about stopping the flow of blood."
"And the cessation of bleeding can be difficult in many cases due to the small size of the vein and the possibility of obstruction by the limb of the arm."
"But phlebotomy on the basilic vein is beneficial for removing blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of branches of the basilica and branches of the cephalica, as we have said."
"And therefore, one should be careful during phlebotomy of that vein, because below it is a nerve; and if the nerve becomes attached to the puncture of the phlebotomy, then numbness will occur, and healing will be difficult, and perhaps not complete. And this nerve can often be felt; but in some people it may be hidden and subtle and not apparent. Therefore, you should pause your phlebotomy with which you are performing phlebotomy on that vein, and avoid the nerve with your strength."
"And if the vein is between two nerves, then cut across the length of the vein."

"The first thing you should know is that phlebotomy should only be administered for the preservation of health and with hope of benefit and caution against the occurrence of illnesses. And it should be performed on one of the veins that are in the elbow, namely the cephalic, basilic or median vein; and it should be performed at the beginning of spring, after fasting in spring and autumn, and around meals in winter, when signs of repletion appear."
"And phlebotomy should be performed either on the third day or at least three hours after a meal."

"And before phlebotomy, enter the bath if there is no prohibition against it, or exercise somewhat to make the blood more subtle."
"And pause phlebotomy around midday, that is around the hour of tierce, as we have said."

"And at his seat should be present those things used by men with fragrant aromas and odors, and balsams and like substances according to their availability."

The stricture is softened, for the stricture, when not equal, or if there is excess fluid, then prohibit blood flow; or if it's due to softening, then also prohibit blood flow completely.
Regarding the stricture, it's necessary that whoever is to perform phlebotomy should rub their hands together before veins are inflated and clearly palpable.
The phlebotomist should anoint their lancet with a little bit of properly prepared olive oil. Then they should place the index finger of their left hand on that vein where the phlebotomy is desired, allowing the vein to swell slightly and preparing it for percussion, in order to determine which vein is suitable for phlebotomy, namely one that throbs like a heartbeat, not one that is full of windiness and contracts under pressure when the lancet is placed upon it. If the lancet opens the vein too widely, then it is too narrow.
Therefore, it is necessary that the phlebotomist be experienced and attentive to all these causes.
After placing the lancet, if the vein is not filled with blood at once, it's better to withdraw and wait awhile. If after that time it still doesn't fill with blood, then try another spot slightly below or above the original location, but avoid any area that appears inflamed or infected.
If the area is inflamed or the patient feels faint, then it's best to leave them alone for a day or two and not attempt phlebotomy again until they have recovered, as bleeding can worsen an inflammation. Do not strain the limb too much nor attempt bathing after the procedure.
If the blood flows freely, then continue with the phlebotomy.
If the opening is small and blood flows subtly, fearing that not enough blood will be let, then insert the lancet again into the same track but adjust the angle slightly to increase the size of the opening. Do this carefully before the area becomes inflamed, as many men are susceptible to inflammation with even a small incision.
But if the area is already inflamed, then do not insert the lancet again there, for it serves no purpose. Instead, place a piece of lint soaked in olive oil on that spot to alleviate the heat and help cool the blood. It's better to use olive oil than other oils in this location.
Similarly, it's recommended to apply a lint soaked in olive oil during any phlebotomy where veins are not allowed to bleed freely, such as when using a tourniquet or tying off the vein with ligatures. This should be done after tying off one of those veins, as it helps to thin and disperse the blood, preventing clotting.
If a large inflammation develops at the site of phlebotomy, which often happens, especially if not enough blood was let or if the vein was barely opened, then apply cold compresses soaked in a solution of water and salt. Soak a sponge in this mixture, lightly wring it out, and place it on the area for about an hour to help reduce swelling and inflammation as it dissolves the heat.
It's important to do this especially when stopping the flow of blood from that vein with a ligature or cauterization, or when tying off another nearby vein.
If there remains some discoloration or greenness in the area for a few days, don't worry about it. If you wish to apply something to the spot, use a plaster made from aloe and myrrh dissolved together, or apply a compress of mint juice or chamomile juice mixed with a little olive oil.
Many times, inflamed areas and swellings occur after phlebotomy at the temple.
Then place your hand over that area and if it feels soft under your compression, then that swelling is harmful and bad. In this case, be sure to apply one of the remedies mentioned earlier to prevent excessive bleeding from an artery.
Make a plaster with something astringent, like oak bark or witch hazel, to harden the area and promote healing. Then treat it with other suitable remedies until it is fully healed.
It's important to remove blood from every person according to their constitution, the amount of blood they have, and the color prevailing over their blood, as appears.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red. Similarly, if the blood is thick, let it flow until it becomes thinner. If the blood is thin or sharp, allow it to flow until its acuteness is removed.
If someone who is robust and has a strong constitution requires bloodletting on one occasion, then make the phlebotomy incision wider and longer to let more blood flow.
Conversely, for those who are weak or frail, make the phlebotomy cut smaller and narrower, and bleed them in small amounts over several sessions. This is to avoid weakening them further by removing too much blood at once.
It's better to make a transverse cut across the vein for phlebotomy rather than just puncturing it with a lancet.
This method of phlebotomy avoids excessive bleeding and nerve damage, making it more praiseworthy and safer than methods based on width and length.
For those who commonly faint during phlebotomy, it's recommended to treat them beforehand with a small piece of bread infused in vinegar or simple oxymel, lightly wrung out. Then bleed them in three or four sessions over time.
If the person has a cold constitution, then before phlebotomy, have them take bread infused in myrrh syrup or aromatic honey syrup with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger, and similar fragrant ones.
If someone faints during phlebotomy due to losing too much blood, then they should drink beef tea and sweet-smelling syrup afterwards. Use aromatics such as aloe and linseed oil to anoint their feet with it.
Use the other causes mentioned in the division of syncope due to evacuation that were discussed in the chapter on syncope.
For those who want to expand their veins and dissolve their blood according to the second intent (purpose), it's recommended that phlebotomy be done on someone whose constitution allows for significant evacuation, but whose strength is weak. This way, the blood is removed gradually over several days, according to the amount of blood that can be safely removed based on their constitution.
For those who wish to expand their veins and draw off their blood according to the second intent (purpose), and whose constitution is robust, then do so about eight or nine hours after the first phlebotomy.
For those who wish to remove blood from their body in opposition to its natural flow (as in cases of left-to-right shunts), it's recommended that they do so on the second or third day after vein expansion.
If there is a small amount of thick, sharp blood in the body that causes fever, it's necessary to remove a large quantity of blood in one session, and make the opening wide enough to induce syncope. However, be sure to maintain all conditions for safe phlebotomy, and place your hand over the pulse of the vein to prevent accidental death instead of fainting, as many untrained or negligent practitioners cause this very mistake.
Do not attempt to redraw blood after the vein has been tied off and it's difficult for blood to flow out. Instead, press on the area with considerable force and curve your hand slightly outward, as this is much better.
To avoid phlebotomy being performed a second time due to inadequate blood flow, you can:
If the vein is infected, then let it be and do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided.
If there is a need to draw blood for evacuation according to necessity, and it is not an excuse, then you should draw that blood from the patient's arm or another vein in their other arm.
The vein called the "funis of the arm" is phlebotomized in place of the cephalic or basilic veins when they are not visible or obscured because it consists of those.
The quality of phlebotomy on the patient is that they should insert their hand into warm water until the tourniquet tightens and the vein appears clearly.
And it should be pressed firmly over that limb with a tight bandage; and phlebotomy should be performed transversely in a small area, not laterally or along the length.
The opening should be wide and the phlebotomy incision should be made where you perform phlebotomy on the junction of the hand, a little bit.
And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then you should immerse your hand in warm water again and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your purpose.
But if you are in the summer days, then you may be excused from immersing your hand in warm water, and in wintertime, the body is naturally accustomed to having blood warmed by hot water.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than that of all other veins because there are no pulsating veins or nerves in it.
And phlebotomy of the little saphenous vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver diseases.
The quality of phlebotomy of that vein is that you should press the hand with a bandage or your hand, after immersing it in warm water until the vein becomes inflated and clearly visible. Then perform phlebotomy transversely; if you mistakenly cut something else, it will not matter much.
And be careful not to compress your hand during phlebotomy because the nerves lie close beneath the skin and can easily be injured.
And again immerse the hand in warm water and let the blood flow into it because if you do not immerse it in warm water, the blood will coagulate at the opening of the vein and prevent its flow.
And once the blood has been extracted according to necessity, then you should press over the vein with oil and salt so that it does not heal quickly. And do likewise for all veins that are streight.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the median vein of the left hand is that it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen.
And do likewise in phlebotomy of this one as you have done with the other.
And the benefit of phlebotomy from the saphenous vein is for diseases that are in the lower parts of the body, such as those of the womb, and retention of menstruation, and for diseases of the skin and ulcers of old wounds, and similar diseases.
And the quality of phlebotomy from this vein is that the patient should immerse their foot in warm water and rub it until the veins appear clearly. Then bind the junction of the foot with a bandage and locate the opening of the vein near the heel, where many branches of it are found on the surface of the foot.
And then perform phlebotomy on the largest branch or one close to the heel below the ankle joint because that is better and safer.
And if you perform phlebotomy on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that lie close beneath it. And stop your phlebotomy with the instrument you are using for cutting across, so that if you wish to incise deeper you may do so without harming any nerve.
And let the phlebotomy be sufficient.
"Yet if it is prohibited to let blood, then immerse the patient's foot in warm water until the blood flows freely from that area until it is evacuated."
"And if the phlebotomist fails at first attempt, then try again with the flebotomy on another vein slightly lower down, because this location is suitable; and do not fear the patient's malice, but protect yourself from nerves as we have said."
"And similarly act upon the other foot equally."
"And concerning the sciatic vein, its location is below the ankle joint near the starting point of the fibula."
"And the benefit of phlebotomy for him is pain relief when it is done to extract blood."
"And the quality of phlebotomy for him is that the patient enters a warm bath, and swiftly and strongly presses his hand to his side above the ankle bone on top of the four aforementioned veins with a long narrow cloth, so that nothing else appears."
"And thus when it appears, then you phlebotomize that vein according to each vein as much as is possible for you and according to the cross-section, which is better; or else incise that vein with an incision or sever it with a section, because its location is suitable."
"And it is in many men much enlarged; and if you happen upon that one, and none of the rest appear to sensation, then phlebotomize some branch of it, namely that which appears on the back of the foot towards the aforementioned heel and ankle."
"And protect yourself from nerves, and let flow as much blood as you wish."
"And immediately untie the ligature and pause upon the location of the phlebotomy wound, and strongly press the location because it is healed lightly."
"Regarding ventosities (flatulence) and their qualities of use."
Winds are of horns, or of ears, or of veins.
And bloodletting is done in two ways: one through scarification and extraction of blood, the other without scarification.
And this bloodletting that is done without scarification has two methods to it, for either fire is used or none at all
And of the fourteen locations on the body where bloodletting is performed with scarification and extraction of blood, one is at the nape of the neck which is the last section of the head; another in the middle of both parts of the collarbone; another under the chin near the jawbone below the mouth; another on both spatulas; another on Alhocoster which is above the hip joint; another on both foci which are located on the forearms; another on both kneecaps; and another on both thighs.
And bloodletting does not draw out the blood from large veins but only from the small capillaries scattered throughout the flesh, and therefore it does not lose its virtue through this method, just as there is no loss of fluid through phlebotomy. Nor should bloodletting be done with fire or without fire in any disease caused by excess within the body until all of the body has been evacuated.
But if bloodletting is necessary for an illness or for cleansing, it should be administered at the beginning, middle, and end of the month, and at any time when necessary.
And therefore when it is necessary in any of those causes, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, we command bloodletting to be done at that time because those days have passed two or three hours.
And it is beneficial to perform bloodletting from the nape of the neck when it causes heaviness in the head and makes the eyes grow dim; but it is necessary that this be an appropriate evacuation for the entire body.
And this bloodletting is done at the location of phlebotomy from the cephalic vein.
But it is not proper to use this method in those who are of cold temperament or those who have catarrh, as it causes great harm to them.
And therefore bloodletting should not be used for the elderly and those whose illnesses are hot in nature.
But it is said that continuous bloodletting causes forgetfulness. Therefore, you should command the phlebotomist to pause his hand slightly at the beginning of the bloodletting in order to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.
But bloodletting of the armpit is done at the location of phlebotomy from the dark vein and the basilica vein: and therefore it causes asthma, and constriction of the lung, and division of the chest muscle of the lung, and coughing, and congestion.
And therefore bloodletting from the armpit should be done gradually, because if it is paused suddenly at the beginning, it generates weakness in the body and stomach.
And bloodletting from the elbow causes accidental pains in the head and inflammation of the eyes, migraines, and pleurisy, and pains in the roots of the teeth. It is done at the location of phlebotomy from the basilica vein.
And you should command the phlebotomist not to press his hand during scarification so that he does not injure the artery and cause bleeding.
And bloodletting from the chin causes dryness in the mouth and corruption of the gums, and it resembles the appearance of illnesses that are in the mouth. It is done at the location of phlebotomy from the lymphatic vessels that are on both lips.
But bloodletting that is done over the two spathula bones causes headache, which occurs due to congestion of heat.
And bloodletting from the two divisions of the jawbone of the face causes benefit in phlebotomy of three veins: the basilica vein, dark vein, and cephalic vein. This is because that attracts blood from all those veins that are large in the flesh, and also attracts the small veins from the other veins that are larger towards reaching the attraction to the three veins.
And command the phlebotomist not to proceed with scarification because the location is bare of flesh and thus exposed to nerves and arteries.
And it is one bloodletting method in which bloodletting is done over the buttocks, causing hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcers that are also there.
And you should command the phlebotomist to do a large bloodletting with a wide-mouthed cup because that location requires strong sectioning. And few thin-walled cups are drawn and it is scarified with scarification.
But bloodletting from the calves decreases by degrees in order to manifest diminution, because it attracts blood from the entire body and causes old pains of the kidneys and womb, and of the bladder. It also provokes menstruation, and causes boils and swellings, and it is done at the location of phlebotomy of two saphenous veins, and the ankle bone, and two small veins.
But they greatly weaken the entire body and cause syncope in many people.
And bloodletting from the ankle bone has benefit similar to that of bloodletting from both calves.
And the quality of the position of bloodletting is that you should pause the cup at the beginning empty and follow by moderate suction, not prolonging the position of bloodletting but pausing it lightly and removing it gently so that the humors come to the location by equal coming. And do not cease doing this until you see the location already reddened and swollen, and that the appearance of blood appears.
Then you scarify and continue suction little by little, and carefully consider the condition of the body.
Because that person who has tender flesh with few pores should be scarified only once and not more than that, so that the location does not become ulcerated.
And command the phlebotomist to widen the scarification a bit and then press gently while maintaining suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then you should scarify twice because in the first instance the way for subtle attraction of blood and wateriness of it is opened. But in the second one, the extraction of thick blood is acquired through scrutiny.
And if the blood is very fetid, then no scarification should be done on the third occasion so that it can conclude.
Finally, when we want to extract blood little by little or a little at a time, one scarification is sufficient for us.
And if we want to extract much blood, we should scarify with great scarification; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary for us to scarify with a previously opened scarification.
And the term of scarification refers only to the depth of the vein.
"When the chain (intestine or hernia) is thick, hard, dark, and its openings are narrow, it is necessary that the places for the cups be anointed with lenitive, aperitive, resolutive; if it is in summer time, thus as oil of aloe or violet oil, or dossy mallows oil, or cooked cumin oil; and if it is in winter time, then oil of narcissus, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or blood, for those resembling this."

"What is it necessary that the person about to undergo cupping should guard before cupping."

"The cups that are not followed by scarification are those that are paused on the liver, and on the spleen, and podagra (gout), and on the navel, in the joints of the ankles, and on the hollow of the ankle, for these members do not suffer scarification upon them."
"And those are not desired except for the attraction of blood from the member, such as pausing cupping on the breast in illness of bleeding of blood from the nose; and they use it so that frozen cold cupping may be dissolved from the member, such as pausing upon the belly and the navel; because it rarifies the member, scalds it, and relaxes the pain because it resolves cupping."
"And cups are paused upon the lungs when there occurs in them opacity or phlegm because by the virtue of strong attraction it draws out opacity and expels phlegm from its place."
"And similarly, it does when paused upon the liver and spleen, because it removes cold cupping from those enclosed."
"And these cups are administered only with suction; some times they are administered with fire."
"And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in the illnesses of the spleen."
"This is why the cup should be filled and made larger, because cold water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been cooked."
"Form of cupping administered with fire: the opening of the cup should be as large as two fingers wide, according to how we have formed it; and the quantity of the mouth should be half a palm wide; and it should be placed on the side where there is swelling, in the location that is in the circuit, half of it, let the traction be small according to the amount that enters into it through a needle."
"It should be made of earthenware or glass, with thick walls, lion's foot, round, and smooth, so that it does not injure the member by its position upon it."
"And in the middle of it should be placed a transverse iron or earthenware wick, whereupon candle is paused with fire."
"And this cup should be much larger than that which we have recommended; and the smaller one according to the illnesses and the condition of him who uses it."
"The cups for infants and emaciated people are different from those for young people and robust individuals."
"The quality of pausing this cup with fire upon the member is that it should extend the mouth of the fire with decently dried wood before a small candle of wax, and pause that upon the middle of the transverse wick in the middle location of the cup, so that the fire may be kindled around the partition of the edges so that not the body of the sick person may be burned."
"And then it should be paused upon the member of the sick person, and let him hold that traction which we have said, until when we have the cups and do not need to hold the wick, because the vapor will rise over that traction, at that time the cup will soon be dissolved."
"Then immediately extinguish the mouth according to its manner, and repeat it if necessary."
"However, in cupping that is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is neither transverse wick nor traction, and it is not administered except that it should be filled with water, and it should be paused upon the member only."
"And this is its form: The larger this cup is, the better, because it can hold a large quantity of water."

"And thus begins the third section of the treatise: On Restoration." "This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine, and concerns the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones."
"O son, know that boastful physicians and common folk, who have never regarded the books of the Ancients nor read even a single letter from them."
"And for this reason it happens that the manner of the Ancients is not practiced in our land, and I do not wish to have been forgotten at any time; nor have I added anything below except by way of condensation for those who read the books of the Ancients and study them diligently, so that they may extract science from them."
"And therefore I direct you to experiment and study throughout the length of my life."
"And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science contains and on which my experiment has passed, since here I have condensed it for you other physicians and freed you from the occupation of elongation; and I have condensed it to the end of condensation; and demonstrated it to the end of demonstration; and formed many forms of instruments that are administered therein, as is done by addition of demonstration, just as I have done in two preceding chapters."
"Chapter One."
"Summaries and collections from the essence of fractures of bones, from which a premise is necessary before we begin to remember the members that are broken in diverse ways one by one." "It is fitting that I should declare in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of my discourse, and the chapters which are intended for the understanding of those who will practice this noble art and who will stand firm on its truth; so that you and they may be prompt to know or learn it better than others."
"And therefore I say that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or collapse occurs, it is necessary for you at the beginning to perform phlebotomy on him or to alleviate his pain by some means, if no prohibition exists such as debility of power or if he is an infant or elderly or during intense heat or cold."
"And furthermore it is suitable to be content with light foods of fresh herbs and the flesh of birds and crabs; and prohibit heavy meats and filling foods and all foods that fill the veins with blood, lest you be assured of a safe wound and lest you fear effusion of matter into the member."
"And therefore return the patient to his first regimen with which he is accustomed to live."
"And therefore, when the broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the patient be sustained with nutritive foods of much and heavy nutrition in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, rice, capons, kid, fish, calf's belly, fresh cheese, thick wine, and similar foods; and with this regimen coagulation of the fracture occurs more gently and easily."
"And know that the bones of children and old people are not able to be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the drying out of the members thereof."
"But nevertheless nature adapts and connects itself over the broken bone in any way whatsoever, with some cause similar to glue that adheres and strengthens it until it is conglutinated together, until the end of fortitude and firmness comes, just as fire at the beginning, unless some cause prohibits it from its works."
"And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the foods of the patient, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said."
"And know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all other members are diverse among themselves."
"And I come with remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in its own chapter separate from the others."
"Great diversity within itself." "And the species of bone fractures are diversified within themselves; for there is an arched fracture other than stellar ones that occur therein; and sometimes they are fractured according to the length of the bone: these are called 'sagittal' and 'stellate' and 'additional' and 'non-additional' fractures, as happens when a reed is prepared to write with, and there is a fracture with swelling and disruption at the head, and there is a small fissure fracture."
"And for each species there is some specific pain or torment in its restoration, which will be remembered distinctly in their proper places."
"Many signs of bone fracture." "And for this reason a bone fracture is known: its torsion and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and its shortening when you compress it with your hand."
"And therefore when there is no apparent torsion in the place nor is any made by your touch when you agitate the bone and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here."
"It is possible that there may be some torsion or small fissure, therefore you should not move it with your hand nor compress it all at once; but pause over it and apply the medications of which remembrance will come to you at this moment appropriate for the place; and furthermore it should be straightened by means of subtle manipulation."
"And know that the place where it is arched and not cut in two or otherwise than sagittally, when all separation from its companion has been removed, then you should begin immediately to correct it, provided that it does not have an open wound."
"And if it has an open wound, then leave it for some days until the wound stops; and furthermore elevate it in whatever manner you can of elevation."
"And know that restoration of it and approximation of it is easier than the bone where there has been separation."
"And furthermore strengthen it according to what will be remembered about it."
"But if there is a separation of the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from both sides if it is the arm or leg; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two strings or with one string and your hand."
"And be in such a position that you hold the member in its natural shape while the separated parts of the broken member are extended; then it is necessary for you to return those additions with force and according to whatever manner you can place them back in their places."
"Be mindful of your strength so that you do not harm the patient through your actions or cause them injury; and proceed with caution according to your ability, applying one bone with its companion according to their best forms."
"Ensure that at that opportune moment you touch the patient with your hand, and if there is any diverse cause here, correct it and equalize it according to the quantity of your strength."
"Avoid violent extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, causing an abscess to form through their actions or causing contraction in the limb."
"And the quality of the bandaging of the injured limb is what I recommend. For you should know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and greatness and in their shape."
"And for those that are small, such as the arm, hand and foot, and similar ones, it is necessary that the wrapping be made with very soft cloth."
"However those that are large such as the thigh or leg, it is necessary to have a thick and hard wrapping in them, for the hard bandage connects the large member strongly on all sides with equal tension so that nothing enters between them."
"And appropriately begin by applying your bandage with pauses in equal parts over the location of that fracture with three or four wrappings according to how much the limb deserves, and strengthen your hand a little with the bandage."
"And appropriately go beyond that to the highest part of where the fracture is located and strengthen your wrapping more than before with which you have strengthened the location of the fracture." "And appropriately extend yourself with wrapping beyond the location of the fracture a bit and slowly loosen your tension little by little until you reach halfway."
"And appropriately take another bandage and wrap it around the location of the fracture again with wrapping, and then go beyond that to where there are no wrappings. And your operation should be in the tensioning and loosening of the wrapping according to what we have said about the first wrapping above."
"And appropriately place cotton or cloth between the wrappings to equalize any twisting of the fracture if there is twisting. And if not, do not place anything in between them for any reason."
"And appropriately place another bandage over it and appropriately apply poultices made of suitable herbs at once if there is no inflammation or abscess. And then place something over the wound that will absorb the inflammation, and leave it for a few days. And then strengthen your poultice over it."
"And the poultices should be made of soft white lint cloth and properly formed. Or they can be made from oak wood or palm fronds, and each poultice should have this size and shape:"
"But the poultice that is placed on that fracture should be thicker and softer than the other poultices. And its length should be equal to the size of the limb in both magnitude and smallness. And appropriately strengthen your wrapping over the poultices with another bandage according to your first tensioning. And appropriate tie it down from above with a thread, as we have said about tensioning, so that your tensioning is greater where the fracture is located, and the farther away from the fracture, the less tension there should be."
"And the threads should be soft in size and fineness, and made of wool. And if the threads are thick, such as I have seen some people use who tie their threads with twisted cords, it is a great error when they try to make the tension even."
"And very fine threads are not convenient because you will not be able to achieve the tension that you want with them. And it is necessary that there is less than a finger's breadth between each poultice."
"And if the pain is felt at the ends of the poultices due to tensioning in those places, then place cotton or wool between them so that it does not cause injury."
"Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by swelling, and there can be disruption of the nerve in the cord. And I have discussed this in more detail in chapter two on singularities."
"And know that not every broken limb should be strapped with poultices on the first day. The reason for this is that when a limb is large, it does not necessarily need to have poultices placed on it until about two or three days after, depending on your certainty of the arrival of an abscess."
"And this is the manner in which the ancient surgeons treated fractures and dislocations using poultices that were placed on them."
"The manner of poultice used in the treatment of fractures, convenient for many complexities and especially for women and children, because it does not cause heat or friction. And you should take powdered mortar, which is the ground flour that comes out of the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill."
"And mix it in this way: sieve it with albumen from an egg, and use that mixture without making it too thick or too thin."
"Another way to make a poultice for treating fractures and dislocations is: Take asafetida, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mustard, which is the resin of wild mustard, two drachms each; myrrh and aloe, one-half drachm each; alochil, twenty drachms; all are ground and sieved and mixed with bitter almond water or egg white if the condition is scalded. And use this poultice because it has a good composition and restores lightly broken bones, and is suitable for many people."
"Another way to make a poultice suitable for fractures and twists is: Take mustard and white lead, two drachms each; myrrh and aloe, one-half drachm each; acacia, six drachms; gum arabic, twenty drachms."
"All are ground in a mortar with a mallet and then sieved, mixed with water or egg white, and applied."
"A way to make a poultice for joints and separated bones in their places, and when there is accidental pain in those areas and for cracks and fractures: Take clean wool and immerse it in vinegar and hot oil and place it on the location."
"But this poultice is not useful for healing, bringing an abscess to a head, or expelling pain properly."
"Another way to make a poultice that restores broken bones is: Take fig leaves from the fig tree and leaves of wild poppy. Count them all together and make a poultice of them."
"An abbreviated method of administering a poultice for the restoration of bone fractures and when you want to resolve the remnant of an abscess: Take roots of white lead, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, oat flour, all in equal parts; grind them all together and mix with wine if the member is not scaled."
"If it is scratched, then cleanse it with warm water infused with coriander or simply warm water."
Another method for applying a poultice that is stronger in resolution than the first one, when an abscess occurs during the restoration of the limb: take root of marshmallow and seed of it, and balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all finely chopped and mixed together; let them be crushed and cooked with water from the oak tree or sweet water or wine, everything according to the quality of the limb and its color.
But Hippocrates does not specify in his book that it should be applied on the member for forty days during its restoration, but rather cerote made from wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be between thickness and subtlety.
But it is seen that Galen says they should be applied on the broken member during its restoration and the causes in which there is drying with little heat are such as myrrh, incense, and similar to those causes that we have recommended.
Another way to make a poultice that alleviates injury and pain: take the following finely chopped ingredients and mixed together: wax, celandine, human skin shavings or bird feathers, marshmallow root, and salt; grind them well and sieve them; then cook it to make the poultice.
But the quantity of time that it should remain bound according to what is appropriate and when it should be unwrapped depends on whether pain or swelling has occurred or not; if the bone is broken, it is not advisable to remove it from its place too quickly. Therefore, do not untie it for many days unless there is no pain or inflammation. And if great pain or swelling or inflammation occurs at the place, then you should immediately remove it and apply another poultice to that area.
And take some linen cloth and sea sponge dampened in hot water, and with that wash the affected area inside until the swelling subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the limb rest for an hour. Next, apply over it a compress soaked in sour wine and oil or rose oil to help draw out any accumulated pus and reduce inflammation of the limb, ensuring that there is no further pain.
And then apply another gentle bandage and small poultice over the first one, tightening it gently until it is healed.
And if at some point the abscess, heat, pain, inflammation, and all other symptoms have been completely alleviated, and you still need to apply a poultice and bandage, then do so in the same manner as you did initially.
And if no other cause from those that we have mentioned occurs at the limb, then you should not remove the bandage until three or four days have passed; sometimes even up to twenty days depending on how the limb appears when it is unwrapped, as we have said, ensuring that the fracture adheres and comes together with the coagulation of the flesh above it.
And then adjust the bandage more tightly than the first one, according to how the limb appears when it is unwrapped, and adapt it to the thickness of the swelling of the injured area.
But if the area of the fracture becomes dry and emaciated more than necessary, then you should know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore, immerse that area in hot water every time you remove the bandage; do this for three days altogether. Then loosen the bandage a little, which will allow nourishment to reach the limb and promote healing.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do when it is not set correctly from the beginning, setting it crookedly according to their own error and causing great fear, this has been greatly condemned by ancient authors in their writings.
And I have not seen anyone do such a thing, so it is more reasonable that they should not do it.
Chapter According to: On fracture when it occurs in the head.
The varieties of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and their causes are distinct. For some fractures occur from blows that break the bone entirely into the cavity, leaving the bone like a splinter, which is why this type of fracture is called 'disjointed'; or the blow cuts through part of the bone without cutting through its depth, and this kind of fracture is called 'elevated'.
The plague of these two fractures is great or small, and [it causes] fracture that is crushed or twisted; the cause of it is blunt impact with little force.
And this fracture is penetrating and approaches the muscle which remains on the bone, or it is located on the surface of the bone, with the plague being large or small.
And there is a fracture that becomes concentrated within the bone itself, in the finest hairs of the bone, which is why this type of fracture is called 'capillary fracture'.
And there is fracture caused by a blow or impact that forces some portion of the bone into its interior, which happens in concave locations like those of an egg when it receives an impact and its shell breaks inward.
Especially this occurs in curved bones such as the bones of the head.
Broken bones of all kinds are weighed down similarly to stars separated from their orbits. And you shall return everything in its place.
We recognize all kinds of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry with gentle touches and observation of decayed flesh that is over them.
A capillary fracture is recognized by its appearance on the bone, it is cleaned and rubbed with touch, for where there is a fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, one must first consider the accidents of the disease, and then if they occur due to those accidents that have manifest significance, such as vomiting from cholera, extension, loss of consciousness, fainting spells, acute fever, sweating of the eyes and redness of them, and similar appearances in major dispositions.
But if accidents do not occur frequently, then you await health, but then you begin the treatment of it.
The reason for this is that if it has happened to you at the beginning as it was happening and this is during the winter days, then it is necessary that you study the bone structure before the fourteenth day according to all dispositions.
And if it is during the summer days, then you must extract the bone before the seventh day, having ensured that what remains of the bone is corrupted by the paniculum, and that the accidents which have been mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the paniculum covering the brain and is with small pieces and attrition, then it is necessary that you insert the comminuted fragment and attrition according to what I will recommend to you.
And that you shave the head of the patient and uncover the bone according to your custom and manner as much as possible according to the figure of the fracture, so that it is light on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you must perform the detection of the bone, fluxes of blood or a hot abscess, then you must pause for it with what is necessary, and this is that you wash the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and make the fracture rest until the abscess is sedated, and be sure of fluxes of blood; and then immediately begin with the circumcision of the bone and the ablation of it.
And this is according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you insert the bone with a narrow incision such as this: and immediately administer another incision tool, somewhat wider than the first: and this is its form:
"And then administer more of the incision powder than the second one."
"And it is necessary that you have various incision knives of different lengths, some of which are thicker than others; some of them are sharper than others and are very sharp at their tips, and they are made of iron or steel."
"And use them with ease in percussion on the incision to avoid hitting the head and harming it."
"And if the bone is strong and hard, then you must first perforate around it before administering the incision knives, which are called non-depth knives."
"And they are not called thus because those do not reach the end of the bone where it is appropriate to use them, so that the knife has a rounded tip or edge on that side which is left behind, similar to a small margin or circle, which prevents being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone."
"And it is convenient that you take many of these knives, each one suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that in all tests of the brain, the tip is sharp, and its length and width are according to the thickness of this test."
"And this is the way of three types of knives:
"You are to now cut with the incissors whatever is between each joint of the bone, and you shall gather that thin pus which you can raise by levitation, as we have said before, so that you eradicate the bone, either by hand or by some other cause using certain instruments which you shall have prepared for this purpose, such as tweezers and subtle forceps."
"And be sure that the scissors do not touch any part of the skin."
"And then when you have circumcised the bone and separated it from the skin if it is continuous with it, you shall extract it, but first scrape and clean that in its roughness which is in the bone with another instrument similar to scissors, but nevertheless ensure that it be thin and subtle."
"And if there is any fracture of the small bones and you try to remove them with ease using whatever instrument is necessary, then clean the wound with linens and bandages which we have mentioned."
"And another method of operation is light and distant from fear, which Galen describes as worthy of great praise; this is the manner of it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin uncovering the fracture of the bone in the place where the break is most evident and manifest, until when you have uncovered that fracture and paused with the end of this gentle and happy scissor which has this shape:"
"And the round end is lion-like and does not cut any skin, while the sharp end is like this in two sides lengthwise, thus; so that the round end fits against the skin with the sharp edge striking the bone."
"And then strike the scissors with a small hammer on one end until you cut all of the bone with ease as it revolves, and you are certain that the skin does not tear in any way whatsoever, and if the patient is the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, he was asleep."
"And then if any skin remains attached to the bone in certain places of the bone, cut that with the round end of the scissors and remove it easily, for it is removed without injury or fear."
"And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the skin, and what is cut off or removed from the bone is only its surface, and remains rough and sharp in places, then you must shave that roughness and remove those sharpnesses with subtle razors which you have prepared in different sizes so that it may be possible for you to use them in all places where they are most convenient and straight according to what you perceive the operation of the broken bone and its figure to be."
"And you must use in your shaving of the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors, and then immediately use the most subtle of them, and do not cease doing so continually until you have administered to it the thinnest and the most subtle of all."
"But in each of those other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what you perceive about their correction."
"And it is because not all those who have studied this art agree on this." "And we have shown in large fractures what has been written about this."
"And if the skin is torn when you remove the bone, then you must take cloth according to the quantity of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the opening of the wound; and also take another doubled or tripled cloth, dip it in wine and rose oil and place it on the first one and do this as lightly as possible without pressing too hard on the skin; then apply a linen bandage over that and don't pull it too tight but only to the extent that the wound itself requires."
"And leave it with this treatment for one or two days until you are sure that the wound is dry and cauterized; then remove it and apply medicines which promote drying, such as linseed oil and powder of iris, and powder of aloe and tragacanth, and others like them."
"And if you make these medicines into powders that are finely ground and applied dry to the wound; generally speaking, apply any medicine that has the property of cleansing without biting."
"And be sure that the wound in your treatment is kept clean and not covered with grease or anything else; do not let it become infected; because when an infection sets in on the skin of the brain it corrupts it and causes illness, even death."
"And if an inflammation occurs on the face of the head, especially after the bone has been exposed and when its treatment is neglected, it appears black on its surface; then consider that if such accidents occur in the illness they are harmful without doubt."
"If blackness does not appear on the face but only from the medicine applied to it, and this is due to the virtue of that medicine, then you must take a portion of honey and three portions of rose oil, mix them with linen cloth and apply it to the face; then treat it with the appropriate remedies until it heals."
"Chapter III"
"On restoring the nose when it is crushed."
"You should know that the nose is not broken except when two parts are crushed simultaneously and raised up, or one of those bones is displaced; because there are two bones in the nose, the left one being made of cartilage and not broken, but only bruised and twisted."
"But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must insert your little finger into the nose and reduce the fracture with the tip of your thumb inside and your other fingers outside until the nose returns to its natural shape." "And have this done easily by you, taking care not to cause harm to the patient during the operation."
"And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you must use the tip of a probe with a small thickness."
"And if the fracture is in both parts, then you must perform this same operation on both."
"And be careful to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; otherwise, wait until the seventh or tenth day after the wound has healed."
"And also insert into the tip of the nose a wick made of cloth if the fracture is in one part or two cotton balls if it is in both parts; and they should be as thick as possible to fill the cavity of the nose."
"Some of the ancient restorers say that the cotton balls should be wrapped in wool and changed every day, but I have not seen this; however, it is necessary that the wick be wrapped in egg white mixed with powdered mallow; then leave it until the bone has healed and the cartilage has hardened."
"Sometimes, instead of cotton balls, it is appropriate to insert pieces of lion's claw into the nose, especially when there is abundant pus and inflammation in the fracture of the nose, provided that the patient can breathe freely."
"This method is not necessary for you; if you wish, do this, or if you wish, use cotton balls instead."
"And if inflammation occurs again after the wound has healed, plaster the nose with cerote or cotton infused in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon."
If there is no aposteme, cauterize it first with flour of sesame and powdered earth of iris mixed with egg white; then place a lion's skin on him, and do not allow him to raise his nose for any reason.
If the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then you should apply splints to them and extract those that need it using appropriate instruments; then close the wound and treat it with something that adheres and consolidates, and use plasters suitable for that purpose.
If plague occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with hot compresses and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is fractured.
When the lower jawbone is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by plague, first examine whether the fracture is on the outside only, and whether it is not broken in two and curved inward at its edges. If so, its treatment will be easier.
Then, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth and similarly for the left side with the right index finger. With this finger, gently push the bone edges together from inside while pressing the outer part of the bone together with your other hand until it becomes evenly aligned.
And if the jawbone fracture is already separated into two pieces, then you should apply traction to both parts in a straight line until it is possible to achieve alignment.
And if there is commotion and displacement of teeth, then strengthen those that you expect will break with gold or silver wire or thread. Then place a roller bandage on the broken jawbone, and place another roller bandage over it doubled. Then place a large, well-made splint on top, or use a piece of sole leather cut to the length of the jawbone. Then tie everything securely in place according to how you think is appropriate for that application, taking care not to cause further damage.
Command the patient to rest and avoid physical exertion, and have them take liquids slowly through a straw
And if you estimate that the bone has already begun to change shape in some way, then on the third day remove the splint and realign it. Then apply plaster made from powdered brick, egg albumen, or chickpea flour over the splint, taking care to first remove the cerotum. Then place a linen bandage soaked in oil over the plaster.
And when the plaster is well attached and adjusted onto the bone, and there is no further displacement of the bone position, leave it in place until the fracture has healed and strengthened. For many times this fracture becomes solid in three weeks.
And if another boil occurs later on, then apply whatever has been mentioned many times in the treatment of it until it is healed and the boil has disappeared.
And if the fracture is accompanied by inflammation, then consider whether it is due to a separated bone fragment or infection. If there are infected areas, then use whatever you think appropriate to remove them using suitable instruments.
"If the opening of the wound is too narrow, then widen it using forceps according to your needs."
"And when you have finished those procedures and there is nothing left to remove, then if the opening of the wound is wide enough; otherwise, apply one of the plasters that are suitable for this purpose and cover it until it heals."
"Chapter Five"
"The repair of the collarbone when fractured."
"Often the clavicle is broken near its middle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways."
"When it is broken or separated, or separated into two parts without accidents, its repair is easy; when it happens as described in these procedures, its repair is difficult; when the fracture has an opening."
"And the procedure for this is that when the fracture has no opening, you should have two assistants present: one to hold the broken clavicle in place and the other to extend the neck at that point."
"And then approximate the fracture with the aid of those said above until it takes on the correct shape, and is not in too much prominence or concavity."
"And if you need more extension, then place the palm of your hand on the sick person's chest with a cloth or wool between them, the size of which should be according to the amount needed; and extend and elevate the clavicle and press the cloth with your hand until the fracture is approximated correctly."
"And if you cannot draw the end of the clavicle outwards to its proper position, then have the sick person sit up on their neck and place your palm over the muscle of the right shoulder with sufficient force."
"And the assistant should press on the muscle of the shoulder blade until the bone of the clavicle is elevated to its proper position, and then you should reduce the fracture and approximate it with the aid of these said methods."
"But if there are spurs on the clavicle that are painful, then you should cut over them and remove those spurs with ease."
"And if it is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision into it with one of the knives that you have brought for this purpose, as you had placed the clavicle on the instrument that serves to hold it; and this instrument is made of wood or iron, and its shape is like this:"
"And it resembles a spoon, the concave part of which should be according to your need, depending on the size of the bone or its prominence."
"But its length should be according to what is possible for you during the operation; it should have two extremities of this type, one broad and the other narrow."
"And if the disruption of the wound that you incised when you removed the spur from the bone is large and you are certain that there will be an abscess, then approximate the two lips of the wound with stitches."
"And if the disruption is small or you fear an abscess, then fill the wound with dressings and powders according to the size of the wound."
"And if an abscess forms, then soak a cloth in vinegar, rose oil, and wine and place it on it."
"And all of this should be done without causing pain to the fractured bone; and you should see the patient every day so that when the linen becomes softened or if you see the splint becoming loose, then you should correct this and tighten the bandage."
"And also make the patient sleep on their back and place a small cushion at the level of their shoulder blade during the night so that the assistant's arm is raised to the side and therefore the clavicle fracture is elevated due to the elevation of the muscle; or the patient's arm can be tied to their neck."
"And do not remove the bandage unless it happens that there is discharge in the area or an abscess forms within twelve days."
"And also renew the plaster if you see it becoming wet in that place, and adjust it to the reduction and restore it and leave it until it is restored and the clavicle fracture has healed."
"And it is strengthened and comfortable in about twenty-eight days, but sometimes in some men it can be completed in less time than that."
Chapter VI
Of the Reduction of Shoulder Fracture.
The scapula is fractured on its lateral side and only the margins are broken, not the body itself.
And therefore when it is fractured on its medial side - and it is fractured in the middle of it - and only known by touch, then according to the figure of the fracture you should prepare for it and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way that is possible for you.
And also place powdered flour with egg white and linen cloth on it, and then sprinkle doubled cloth powder on it. Then place a thin board splint according to the size of the whole scapula or a little more on top of it.
But if the splint is concave in some parts of the scapula, then you should fill that concavity with linen cloth and place the splint according to equality.
And also strengthen it from above with a long bandage with appropriate constriction and firmness of the splint so that it is not removed from its place.
Check the bandage every day, and whenever it becomes softened, replace it on another occasion and tighten it and adjust the splint if it has been removed from its place.
Have the patient lie on their side.
The scapula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five days.
And therefore when this number is completed, then you should dissolve the bandage, and know for certain that it is of those bones whose union is not doubted nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed to the site of the humerus and place the elbow outside, then incise it there, leave it open, and do as we have said in the clavicle fracture regarding the treatment of an abscess if one occurs here.
De fractura del colz e de restauracio d’ella.
"For the restoration of a broken foot."
"The foot sometimes breaks in the middle and is displaced; however, its ends are usually intact but crushed."
"And when the middle of the foot is broken in an accident, it declines to the sides and causes great pain, difficulty in walking, coughing, fever, and vomiting blood. The fracture is evident in the cavity of the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation."
"And for the restoration of it, the patient should be placed on their back and a pillow put between their legs. The muscles of the foot should be pressed upon and the bones adjusted with two hands, taking care in the operation and ensuring that everything is done according to the best possible manner and what is convenient, until the bone returns to its proper figure."
"And then apply plaster and cotton wool, and pause on a delicate wooden board or calico cloth, taking care to wrap it in cloth. And take care that it is placed upon the broken bone without moving it; and sometimes bind it around the back with bandages according to its roundness, and strengthen it with appropriate strapping. And also consider the binding at all times, and when it becomes softened, strengthen it."
"And if it is necessary to provoke the removal of that [cast], because of corruption or pressure, pain, or an abscess that occurs in the place, then you cut and untie it, remove the plaster, and rectify what has happened there with a healing manner for it. And also reduce the plaster again, sometimes even its face; and adjust the strapping until it is healed."
Capítulo VIII
Chapter VIII: On the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
"Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in large spaces that are on the back; and at the extremities of their ends, such breaks do not happen unless it is due to them being cartilaginous." "The discovery of it is not hidden from sensation during its investigation with these tools."
"And the restoration should be done in such a way that you align the fracture with these tools according to the most possible manner, ensuring it is aligned properly depending on what is needed, and then apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel if necessary."
"And if the fracture of the ribs is displaced at its ends inside, then great pain and puncture will occur for the sick person, like the puncture that occurs in those who have coughed up blood, and because the bone pierces the pleura. And this causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to cure."
"And even the Ancients used this technique with many tools."
"And among the things they said is this: whenever foods are given to the sick person that causes inflation and windiness, so that the belly becomes inflated and extended, then the fracture will be pushed towards its ends on the outside."
"But we would not recommend this because it could easily lead to an abscess forming if it is not done carefully. And even if it has already happened, then it will help with that."
"And some among them said that it is better to remove the wind and then suck strongly with force; this is similar reasoning, but however it is feared that the wind might drive the superfuities to the place through weakness."
"And some among them said: whenever the place is covered with wool that has already been immersed in warm oil, and powders are placed between the ribs inside until it is full, then the wrapping should be done as evenly as possible according to its roundness; and then the sick person should be treated with dietary cure and medication for pleurisy."
"And if the sick person suffers from severe constriction that cannot be endured, and the bone pierces the pleura with sharp pain, then we fear for the sick person. Therefore we should make an incision over the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place on top of it the instrument called the 'cipher' which was previously mentioned. Then we should carefully insert the bone and remove it."
"And then we should join together the two lips of the wound if it is large using sutures, and treat it with plasters inside until it heals."
"And if an abscess happens inside, then quickly and gently place powders in rose oil on the place, cover it, and treat the sick person with what is appropriate for an internal abscess."
"And let him be propped up on pillows while lying down until he is healed."
"Chapter ix"
"A restoration of fracture of the spinal bones of the back and neck."
"Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and they tend to adhere to each other, similarly to the spinal bones of the back." "And if this happens to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not, then consider this: if sometimes the muscles are already softened, stunned, and dead, and cannot move those parts nor extend them nor straighten them, and when you compress those parts with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they do not feel pain in those parts, then know that it is not healing, and in these conditions it is mortal."
"And if those parts move and feel compression with your fingers or puncture, then know that the spinal cord is saved and the illness will recover with treatment."
"And if you see similar symptoms in the bones of the neck, then consider their weight."
"Now if you see that they are already softened, it has happened to those parts what happens in the muscles, and especially when lying on their back, there is shortness of breath and excretion without control, and sometimes they are suddenly reversed on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then know that it is mortal, and therefore do not bother with its treatment."
"But if none of these things happens, the condition is better and easier to treat."
"And the treatment for this is that you should apply a poultice made of hot cabbage leaves, smeared with honey and oil or crushed mulberries, and place it on the affected bone. Repeat this application several times a day until the swelling is reduced."
"And then apply one of the comforting and heating plasters, and tie it on tightly. Command the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the side where they feel pain until they are healed."
"And if it happens that the bone is fractured or some other cause has already separated from it, then you should dry it with a bandage and expose the bone. Then join together the two lips of the wound with stitches if it is large, and treat it with a liniment of henbane until it heals."
"But if it is the end of the bone that has broken off, then you should insert your left thumb into the anus and grasp the broken bone with your other hand according to its position. Then apply a poultice and plaster, and bind it tightly."
"And if you feel that there are broken pieces within the wound, then remove them and clean out the wound. Then treat it with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals."
"Chapter .10"
"On the restoration of a tooth."
Bone fractures in wide joints are broken, and if they break, the fracture of them is only a communion at the ends of those and are inserted into the length and decline towards the inside; and it happens to the patient pain in that place and puncture, and the limb becomes stunned when it's fractured.
And its restoration is that you stretch your hand over that side which is above the fracture which is its shape.
And if the fracture is only at the end of it, then you should align that according to what appears appropriate for equating an entry that is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or one that already declines towards the inside, then you should make the patient lie on their belly so that you can prepare for the restoration of that fracture. And then once you have aligned it, pause on that plaster, and also pause on that stick of wood or bone, and stretch with strictness over the one where there is no fear of puncture from fracture nor displacement of the splint.
And align the concavity with whatever you use to fill that entry so that it takes on the correct structure, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their side.
But if an abscess forms, then you should cease the extension and restoration of it until the abscess is sedated, and pause on whatever sedates that according to what has been preceded. Then return to the restoration of it, and stretch it as is necessary.
And if this happens in the bone, then you should extract them.
Chapter XI
On the restoration of a fracture of the ankle joint.
"Aid is required between the neck and the top of the shoulder."
"And if it is broken, the restoration of it is done in one of two ways, of which one way is that you take a piece of plaster of moderate thickness and apply it thus:"
"And there should be attached at both ends two ligatures, and nearby suspended from the elevated location."
"Then place the patient on a chair; and nearby, gently place their broken arm on a flat surface so that it reaches its elbow in the middle of where the curvature of the bone is attached."
"And then suspend some weight above that, or the attendant should extend it towards the sides; and immediately the physician should apply the fracture with his hands together, so that the fracture returns as necessary."
"The other way is that the patient should be propped up on their neck and suspend the arm at the elbow with a ligature, and then command two attendants: one of them should hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other should hold below it; and each of them should extend to their respective side."
"And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then strain over the fracture with a ligature and over that again with another ligature, and each of the two attendants should extend to their respective sides."
"And if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the ligature should be placed on the elbow and the other half below the fracture near the joint."
"And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then it is necessary that the ligature should be placed over that location and over that joint."
"Then place it gently and without difficulty, and immediately when it is placed according to what is necessary and applied with a good application, then strain it so that it does not happen that an ulcer forms."
"But if an ulcer forms, then leave the bandage on for up to seven days, and place over it a clean lint soaked in warm water and rose oil until the ulcer has healed, and then strain it."
"And the way to strain it is: place plaster over the fracture, and then wrap a clean napkin over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place his hand covered over his muscle."
"And then wrap the cloth and strain the arm on that support so that the arm is in a stable position, unless some cause prevents it due to the broken bone."
"And then use splints."
"And then place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the others; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with an addition of three fingers on either side."
"And then strain over the splints with the bandage which was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"And ensure that your bandage is as tight as possible around the location of the fracture." "And when the fracture has been reduced, then make the bandage looser."
"And therefore if you see that the situation of the splints and the bandage is as we have said in the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do it."
"And if you fear an ulcer forming, then leave the bandage and splints on for up to seven days as we have said."
"And also consider the ligature in all cases for three days so that there is no pressing or inflammation at the location; otherwise, the food may not reach the limb due to an excess of tightness; therefore correct everything as we have said."
"But if you are in doubt that nothing will happen because of it, then do not remove the ligature for less than three days or more."
"And let his sleeping place be on his back, and let his hands be placed on his stomach."
"And place over the cooking stove full of wool on which to rest the limb, and consider at all times of night or day that the figure of the broken limb is not displaced nor dissolved, otherwise if the ligature becomes moist then correct everything with your own judgment."
"And give the sick person their food according to the order which we have mentioned earlier, ensuring that the food is subtle at the beginning until straining begins, and then it is necessary that you thicken the food for him."
"The custom of the splint and bedding is that they should be strained for forty days, and then it is necessary that they are removed and a bath administered, and treated with ointments which correct it."
"And if the fracture is laid bare, then do not remove the ligature and splints for up to ten days or up to two months."
Chapter XII
"On the restoration of fractures of the arm."
Brace is composed of two bones called radii; one is small and touches the said thumb, the other is large and is placed next to the small radius on its outer side.
When only the larger radius breaks, or when both break together.
And when the small radius bone is broken above, its restoration and healing will be easier
When it breaks below the fracture, the restoration of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse still when both bones break together
Therefore if the bone that is broken is the small radius above, it is convenient for the doctor to apply a small extension with ease while waiting for its healing.
But if the large radius is what's broken, then it requires that a stronger extension be applied.
And if both radii are broken together, then you should apply traction quite strongly
And it is necessary to apply the figure of the hand during the restoration and extension of it stretched out on a splint, with the wrist of the hand placed above all others, so that the ear lobe may be lower than the other fingers, and the patient may be sitting on a cushioned chair.
And there should be a splint placed in front of it at its height or towards it, so that the patient does not run into any trouble.
And moreover the minister should extend the arm of the party above with his own hand or with a sling, and another minister should similarly extend it from above.
And furthermore the doctor should heal the bone while reducing it into the best possible position.
And if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then you should try to reduce them all back into their proper place with your own strength.
