And if there appears in it a splinter separate that punctures the elbow and is not to your expectation in the restoration of it, then you should apply traction on it and reduce that according to the manner which has been described above.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already spoken about that specifically in another chapter, and therefore you should take care of it according to that.
And if an abscess forms at the beginning of your treatment, then you should lay a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and it should be medium between thickness and thinness, and press the cloth on it with subtle tension until the abscess is drained. Then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered mallows with clear egg whites, and also apply bandages; and the bandage that is applied to that fracture should be soft and strong.
And know that in most cases the number of bandages for the arm is two or three, or there may be a fracture at one elbow or at both elbows together.
And moreover you should apply your tension on the location of the fracture with great force and vehemence, and when it is more towards the upper parts above the fracture or towards the lower parts below it, then apply the tension a little softer according to what was preceded in the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, and not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is pressed should be of medium thickness between thin and thick, as has been recommended.
And consider the member and its ligament for a few days. And if some cause occurs that requires correction, such as swelling occurring in the member, then you should immerse the member in cold water until that swelling ceases.
Let the member rest without pressure until it has paused, and then return to applying traction.
"If the swelling has already softened and the bone has moved back into place, then correct this with your skill."
"And consider whether the nutrition reaches the limb due to an excess of narrowing, for then you should soften that a little and let it rest for a few days until the nutrition returns to it, and then strengthen it."
"But if the illness has not occurred due to any of those causes mentioned earlier, then it should be allowed to rest for about twenty days or so."
"And then suspend the limb of the patient with your skill using agitation movements, and have them sleep on their back."
"And know that this fracture takes about thirty days or thirty-two days to heal, or perhaps it will take twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the disposition of the complexion and the dispositions of the virtue."
"Chapter XIII."
"Of the restoration of fractures of the fingers and toes."
"I know that at the tip of the hand and fingers of these carts there is fracture, not just bruising many times seen."
"And when there is fracture or bruising on the hand, it is necessary that the patient lie flat and in front of them place a pillow according to equality, and nearby place their hand over the extended one."
"And then the minister extends the broken bones, and the doctor fits those together that are congruent with good congruence."
"And then it is necessary to apply plaster and gauze, if there is no cauterization; and then place a splint on top according to the quantity of the location, which you have already wrapped in linen cloth."
"But if the fracture is in the parts inside of the palm of the hand, then make a similar sling of cloth, and command the patient to press it against their palm with the fractured hand; also let it be bound with long cloth."
"And let the splint be of leather with cloth in which there is padding, so that the leather adheres to the palm; and bind it according to what is necessary."
"But if the fracture is on the outside parts, then it is necessary to place splints above and also another splint from the side part of the palm, so that the hand is straight and upright; and then apply the bandage, like when wrapping a rat's tail between the said bands."
"And if there is a fracture of any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, fit it properly and bind it to the palm; and if you wish, place a small splint on the outside so that the fracture is elevated and not crushed."
"But if the fracture is in the other fingers, like that of the middle finger or the index finger or the ring finger or the little finger, then fit it and bind it with its own digit; or all can be bound together in continuity, which is better; or place a small splint on top as we have said for the thumb."
"And consider, in your restoration of the fracture, and apply a cauterization if necessary according to what is appropriate for that operation; also consider the position of the abscess and oppose it with what is necessary when any cause of those occurs."
"Chapter XIV"
"Of the restoration of a bone fracture in the thigh."
"The bone in the thigh is often broken, and this is indicated by pain that increases before and during urination."
"And its restoration is performed in such a way that the ligament tightens over the fracture and other ligaments leave the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their stomach and face."
"And first, the surgeon extends all the ligament to its departure according to its quality when the fracture is in the middle of the bone."
"But if it occurs at the upper end of the thigh, then the ligament is drawn towards the hip at the departure of the penis, so that its extension does not occur inwards, and other ligaments leave the fracture."
"And similarly, if the fracture occurs at the hip joint, then the ligament should be drawn towards it, and its extension should be made away from the joints."
"And then the surgeon uses screws on both sides to reduce it according to its natural shape and join the bone with good union. Afterwards plaster and bandage should be applied if there is no inflammation of the limb." "But if inflammation occurs, then leave it for some days until the inflamed area is reduced, and then return to your treatment."
"The bandage should be made in this way: wrap a hard bandage over the fracture and fold it twice or thrice, removing any excess; then wrap the knee so that it reaches the ankle and thigh."
"And then insert a long splint between the thigh and knee near the hip joints, and bring the ends of the splint from above the two parts, and wrap it around the knee and thigh removing any excess bandage."
"And then apply traction on the thigh at the location of that fracture, and apply a weight of one or two pounds on the bone of the knee."
"And then fill the spaces between the knee and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is well consolidated."
"And then begin with a firm bandage on the middle of the limb over the fracture site with three or four layers, and as you move away from the fracture site, make your bandage thinner, lighter, and softer."
"And then extend both ends of the splint towards below until it reaches the heel of the foot, and tie both strings tightly on the other side so that the limb does not move from its place."
"And then leave the bandage on like this so that there is no infection or inflammation of the limb, nor any swelling or redness around it. And if anything occurs, take care to remove it immediately and correct it right away, as we have said many times."
"But if the bone is pressed down and sticks out at one point, then raise that point as much as possible. And if it cannot be raised, then make a hole over it and remove what is causing pressure, and cure it according to what has been said before until it heals."
"And sometimes this fracture of the knee is strengthened with strings in addition to those already mentioned, as we have said in the case of the adjuster and the arm."
"But however, in the restoration of the limb in this way it should not happen that the patient limps; and if it is restored alone without applying the other bandage, then it is necessary for the one who is always lame."
"And know that the knee is strengthened in forty days or a little more or less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions."
"Chapter 15"
"On the restoration of fractures of the kneecap."
"Know that fracture of the shinbone often occurs suddenly, but most commonly it happens through direct trauma."
"But if the fracture has occurred, then it is nothing more than a break or wound in its parts, and this is accompanied by pain or sharp pain, and on top of that there is great swelling."
"The restoration of the patient is such that what has been broken in their limbs should be set back together with these tools, joined and connected as much as possible by equality, ease, and manufacture."
"And then place plaster on that round bone if necessary, and then press down with sufficient force as appropriate."
"And also consider all the arrangements which we have recommended in other fractures, especially those involving pus and similar accidents, as long as it is suitable for this patient to be treated."
"Chapter XVI."
"The restoration of a fractured bone."
"Know that in the shinbone there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called by the name 'shinbone'; the other is slender and is called 'fibula'."
"And also know that in each of the species of fracture that occurs to both bones of the leg, restoration is done similarly to the restoration of the arm, and the operation for it is one."
"And if both bones are broken at the same time, the shinbone becomes completely shattered."
"And if only the thinner bone is broken, the shinbone remains intact in front."
"And if only the thicker bone is broken, it is the outer one that is fractured, and this is due to causes other than those mentioned above."
"Therefore, apply extension, traction, and splinting of the fragments according to the same principle."
"But it is necessary, if the fracture of the shinbone is located having many attempts, that extension be smaller and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue."
"And in the shinbone there is some cause for operation on the arm, which is when you align the fragments and complete all your operation; then you will take pieces of wood from a tree that has been felled nearby, and they should be made like branches of palm; choose those that are not too thick, nor too thin, and their length should be according to the length of the shinbone, from front to back."
"And apply each splint over two wrappings according to its length, and place one on the broken shinbone on one side and the other on the other side, and they should be from front to back, at the height of the ankle."
"And apply the two splints in three locations on both extremities and at the midpoint, for with this structure the shinbone is prevented from bending to the right or left, and it stands in good position."
"And also apply a splint of wood according to the length of the shinbone, and place it on it so that it retains its movement."
"It is especially necessary that you do this in fracture, and when with it there is proper healing."
"And also examine the shinbone every day and study it carefully, and keep it away from infection, inflammation, or other similar causes. And when any of these causes occurs, then apply this again until it is healed."
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot.
"No fracture occurs entirely in the ankle; but fractures occur sometimes in the bones of the foot; and in these of the foot few fractures occur, and they do not occur except in cases of dislocation."
"And if fractures occur in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones become elevated above each other, then the sick person should place his foot on the ground prepared thus while walking."
"And also apply the other foot on what is elevated by those bones and press them down, press hard, align them until they return to their places."
"And also apply plaster and bandages on it, and place a small table with two flat heads on the belly of the foot."
"And also strengthen that [ankle] with the foot with a descending support, since you have wrapped it with cloths and other things of which you have need."
"And when three or four days have passed, then unwrap the bandage, for you will find the bones aligned, either joined together or dislocated, and the operation on these is as you know."
"But if some of them are joined together, then restore those and align them as I have told you and explained to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand." "And also apply splints on the joined fingers according to their length and place them somewhat aside from them. And also place this table which I have recommended on the foot, and strengthen it with descending support."
"But if what is joined together in the bones are two or three or more, then apply splints on each finger according to its quantity wrapped in lion's skin, and strengthen the table at the lowest part of the foot, with one of the heads of the table placed under the belly of the foot so that it retains it well."
"And remember that you should not forget to consider all the causes which are mentioned to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have spoken about, and apply this to every accident as it is necessary."
Chapter XVIII.
Of fracture of the woman's pubic bone, of the hipbone, and of the penis.
"When the pubic bone of the woman is fractured, then apply that in a padded cell, and also incline it somewhat to the side of the back, and let it be held behind."
"And also the midwife presses on the pubic bone and makes it like a sieve; and also the woman should be struck, and little by little raise her buttocks, for all this is forgotten until the pubic bone is placed before like a sieve and the fractured bone returns."
"And also apply powders on the back of it. And when she wants to urinate, move the cot with ease and let her urinate, and reduce it and return it according to the manner in which you mixed it at the beginning, and then return it to its first powder."
"And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for that is restored."
"And if you wish, take a bladder of sheep and strengthen it on the back of it with a reed cane. And insert the entire bladder into the pubic bone and inflate it through the reed by blowing air into it until the bladder is inflated inside the pubic bone, for the fracture returns."
"And also press it with cotton and keep it at rest for some days as we have recommended until it is healed."
"And when the hipbone of man or woman is fractured, then administer in the restoration and reduction of it what we have recommended for the bone of the thigh."
"And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures and which occur rarely, especially to those who are learned and understand this my book; for that fracture teaches you the correct way in the restoration and support of it, and therefore understand well."
"But in the penis of man when it is fractured, take a gourd of dough and place the penis inside it. And also apply over it wrapping with cloth and let it be wrapped, and then let it rest for three days or so until it is healed."
Chapter XIX.
Of restoration of fractures of bones when they are displaced.
It is convenient for that one to whom it happens that a bone is fractured out of place, and especially if the bone is large like the thighbone or the fibula, or similar ones, that you should carefully perform flebotomy on him at once if the conditions for flebotomy are present in him as we have said above.
"And if bloody flux flows out, then it is necessary that you apply pressure to stop it immediately, powdering sage three times on the wound."
"Therefore begin restoring the fracture immediately today and do not delay, lest an abscess occur."
"But if an abscess occurs, then wait to resume the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has softened. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days of all, because you will cause bad accidents."
"But if the bone breaks through the skin and is visible on the shoulder, then it is necessary that you perform reduction promptly and cover it with soft dressings gently and without excessive extension."
"But if reduction is not readily achieved and the bones remain displaced despite your efforts, then reduce them using this instrument: it is made of iron, seven or eight digits long, and its width varies according to the amount of displacement. And thus the physician must have three or four of these instruments ready for use in all kinds of fractures."
"And if it is reduced, and there is a little hardness due to swelling, then apply pressure gently on it at the time of operation."
"And if it is an extremity with sharp ends that are next to one another, and the skin over it is thickened and swollen, then incise it along its thickest part and open it widely on both sides."
"And this is how it looks:"
"Named in Greek 'labre volentz atelarem', it is a rare disease."
"Why should the extremity of the bone elevated on the bony prominence be padded?"
"And press down on it so that it returns to its place, and remain like this for some time."
"And then try to align the ends of the fracture together, and if the fragment is small and does not fit well with the instrument, then insert the end of that bone inside so that the instrument has power over it."
"But if you cannot reduce the bone despite all that has been mentioned, then cut it with whatever instrument you have available among those which have been listed; or saw it with one of the saws however you can."
"And remove any sharp edges and small rough areas that remain on the bone."
"And when the bone is reduced and yet the patient feels strong pain on movement, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position. And therefore do this which will give great relief to the patient."
"When your reduction of the bone is complete and then you apply dressings with black stiptic wine, especially if it is summer time, do not allow the patient to lie on a greasy pillow or any other cause where there might be oil, so that no corruption or decay may occur afterwards."
"And then apply splints at the time of completion of the reduction of the bone, and leave the dressing uncovered enough so that you can insert traction with forceps and use bandages according to the quantity of the dressing."
"And be careful that you do not strain the dressing over the fracture, for many times fools have done this and it has caused harm to their patients resulting in death or corrupting or damaging bone, which is very unhealthy." "And if your reduction of the fracture is light and soft, differing from the usual type of reduction for fractures."
"If the dressing is large or a bad one, and you fear about those unfortunate accidents which have been mentioned earlier, and you find pain in the location, then do not rely on those splints, but make this wrapping of hard cloth instead of splints and bind it tightly with them."
"And when after one or two days the dressing begins to be warm in that place, then you remove the cloth which you had placed there with wine, and apply hot compresses, as we usually do for dressings, thus using such things as treacle and similar substances."
"And it is convenient that you remove the stitches and examine the dressing every morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and it is necessary that the member be prepared in such a way that the corruption runs out of it easily towards the outer parts."
"If many days pass and the dressing is not consolidated nor does it separate from that warmth, then you would know that this is a case of inflammation for which you should examine the dressing with touch." "And of those inflammations, those which are separated and removed and extracted are good, but those which do not separate and putrefy the member and cause pain, you should incise them according to what is possible for you."
"And if anything happens to the dressing such as swelling or blistering, then it is stitches or some other kind of corruption and putrefaction, therefore you should pause for one of those according to what has been described in its own chapter regarding its cure."
"And of this, let my speech be brief and you make your point: when the bone is crushed, it swells up over the member like the bones of the thigh and the forearm and similar ones in large members, and do not attempt to reduce or extract it because many times death has occurred through this; leaving that alone until corruption occurs, for it will fall off by itself after about twenty or thirty days; and then cure the dressing if you see fit, and if not, leave it."
"Chapter .20."
"Of the treatment of necrosis that occurs in the vicinity of some fracture."
Many times this swelling occurs during the process of healing a fracture, especially near joints, and it is caused by the member being in its usual position.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties such as alum, iris, and myrrh. Mix some or all of these with honey, albumen of egg, or vinegar, and apply it on a bandage over the swelling, binding it tightly but not too tight.
Let this dressing remain undisturbed for several days; then remove it and apply another one until the swelling has disappeared. Alternatively, place a lead plate over it, since lead has the property of reducing any swelling in the limbs.
If the swelling has already become hard and it is necessary to provoke a break in it, then cut through the skin along the line of maximum prominence using a scalpel blade. Excise the excess bone and remove any fragments until the swelling disappears. Then cure it with appropriate treatment until it heals completely.
Capítulo XXI
Chapter XXI:
"When a bone fracture is restored, but the member remains delicate and brittle, this occurs only for several reasons: one of which is the multitude of solution of the ligament not according to what is due, or by excess of constriction of the ligaments where nourishment cannot reach the member, or by multitude of useless embroilment; or by superfluous movement not at its proper time; or by lack of blood in the patient's body and debility."
"To cure this, one must give the patient food to make their heart tender and multiply blood in them; they should also administer baths; and apply warmth and joy upon them."
"And then pause the pressure on the member, so that the pressure attracts nourishment to it, or continue rubbing it with warm water until nourishment returns to its normal state."
"Chapter xxii."
"Regarding the treatment of bones that are broken crookedly and then restored, it is prohibited to operate on them for this reason:"
"When the member has been restored but still retains signs of its former crookedness and prominence at the site of the fracture and node, although the member may not be prohibited from its natural operation, you should not accept the claim that it is newly broken."
"But many foolish physicians and restorers have done this in our land, and this operation is greatly to be condemned because it brings great danger with it, the sign of which is death."
"But if the limpness and nodosity are recent, then they should be rubbed with warm water in which softening herbs have been cooked, such as althea flowers and rose hips, and mallow, and similar ones; and a plaster of mollifying paste made from these should be applied, like that called dialaquilon when well-made; or fresh rose hips ground together with honey or oil of cinnamon should be applied as a plaster; or figs steeped in fat of pigeon should be rubbed on, and suchlike medicines named 'amermans consolidacio'."
"And sometimes with frequent light friction it can be dissolved, especially if done with the most humid ones, and movement of the member should be encouraged in all parts at all hours."
"But if the crookedness has already appeared and is strong and hard, and has turned into bone and there is a need for its treatment with iron, then it should be cut away as much as possible above where the continuation of the bone has dissolved, and what is superfluous from the node of that member should be incised with subtle scissors, and ease should be given to it through diligent study."
Chapter XXIII
"Concerning dislocation."
Dislocation is the displacement of certain joints from their proper place, causing them to lose their mobility and altering the shape of the limb, which leads to great pain and injuries for the patient.
Therefore when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to reduce it as soon as possible, and not delay the reduction, because if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and difficult to reduce.
And therefore it is necessary that at the time of inflammation, the joint should not be moved or stretched, because often this causes spasm and severe pain.
But when this happens, then you must cover the patient with a blanket and let them rest until the inflammation subsides. Then the limb should be wrapped in hot water and oil, and finally it should be gently reduced.
And the limb should be treated with whatever will bring the memory of its former position back to mind.
And now I have ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what has been previously stated in fractures, from the division of the body into upper and lower parts.
Chapter XXIV
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw
When both jaws are dislocated, they are not difficult to reduce. Dislocation of both jaws occurs in two ways: either the mandibles are displaced from their normal positions by slight movement and become softened a little; or else they are completely dislocated and must be softened at the point of articulation until saliva begins to flow from the mouth, indicating that it can be opened and closed again. The tongue should be wrapped in cloth during this procedure.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position on its own with no cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then gentle reduction should be administered promptly, without delay.
"And let the attendant hold the patient's head, and the physician insert the tip of his thumb into the groove of the mandibular dislocation inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the others hold the patient's hands from outside with which they stabilize him. And command the patient to cease biting down and relax each side in turn; and the physician guides the mandible back into its proper place."
"And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both mandibles are dislocated at once, then administer evaporation with warm water and oil until it becomes easier to reduce both, and do not prolong the reduction of one side while delaying the other, as we have said."
"And when they return to their proper places and the mouth of the patient is closed but not relaxed, then apply powders made from cloth with wax and rose oil; let them be made of wax and rose oil; and let them be applied with ease using soft linen."
"And let the patient sleep on his back with his head propped up between two pillows, so that he does not bite down on anything at night; do not force him to eat or drink anything. Let his diet be soft food until the pain is gone and the jaws are healed, then he may eat what he wishes."
"And with this administration should be done easily and gently, without forcing the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, or any other purpose until the jaw is healed."
"And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated and do not return to their proper places, then often a fever occurs along with continuous thirst, and sometimes there is vomiting and copious purging."
"And when this happens, know that it is a serious matter, for many people have died from this within eight days."
"Chapter .xxv."
"Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the end of the muscle."
"The collarbone is not dislocated from its inner side due to its continuity with the sternum; however, sometimes it can be dislocated on its outer side, which is noticeable by touch."
"And for the reduction of this dislocation, let the patient lie on his back and extend his arms." "Then press upon it with your palm firmly until it returns to its proper place."
"And then apply plasters and powders and strengthen that."
"But the end of that which seizes the lung and is attached to it does not become dislocated at any time. Therefore, when it needs to be reduced, do so according to what has been said above."
"And then apply plasters, powders, and bandages to that place, and order the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed."
"With this same treatment, the end of the muscle is reduced when it has become separated from its proper place."
"Chapter xxvi."
"Of the reduction of dislocation of a muscle."
"You should know that a muscle can only be dislocated in three ways: one being at the point of attachment to the tendons on either side."
"The second way is when it is dislocated at its midpoint above the muscle, which rarely happens. It is not dislocated behind by the shoulder nor in front through the nerves."
"And most often it becomes dislocated and protrudes at its attachments on either side of the tendon, especially in those with little flesh where it is easily dislocated. But in those with much flesh, it protrudes with difficulty."
"And often in some men there occurs a blow or fall, and the muscle becomes inflamed with an incurable abscess, and it is dislocated by that."
"Therefore, you should first remove that before it becomes entirely hard, and then proceed with its treatment."
"It is recognized that the dislocation occurs at its attachments on either side of the tendon. Therefore, you can distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, for there is a clear difference between them. You will find concavity in the head of the muscle on either side of the tendon, which resembles a chicken's comb. The patient cannot bring his hand to his ear nor move that limb."
"If it is dislocated towards the sternum or upwards, you will find a clear and obvious touch."
"And this dislocation can be easily reduced when it is fresh and the patient is a child."
"And the reduction of this dislocation is performed by raising the patient's hand above his head. Then place your thumbs on either side of the tendon below it and raise the joint with force upwards to its proper place while the patient raises their hand and extends it upwards. Then press upon it with your thumbs as it returns easily."
"But if it does not return as described above and the dislocation has existed from the beginning for many days, then the patient should be immersed in warm water and treated with an embrocation that softens and moisturizes it like this: take a decoction of ale, linseed, and honey mixed together."
"And then the patient should be propped up on his back and wait for it to become loosened between elevation and pressure. Then the physician should place their heel upon the prominence and raise the head of the muscle with force while pulling the patient's hand so that it is not hidden at its attachments, and another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it does not move from its attachments until that muscle returns to its proper place."
"And if you wish, you can reduce it in this way: Have present a man who is taller than the patient and stands on that side. Insert his muscle between the tendons of the patient's dislocated muscle and raise the tendon upwards while the patient hangs suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand from the attachments on their abdomen." "But if the patient is light, then another cause should be suspended with him so that he may hang heavy, for the dislocation returns quickly."
"And it can be reduced in another way: Take a long pole with a round head like a mortar pestle that is neither too thick nor too thin. Place it on the ground and have the patient stand beside it with cloths of lions placed upon its head. Then have the patient stand along the length of the pole, extend their hand to the other side, and lean their body across with force so that the joint quickly returns to its proper place."
"And if it is difficult to reduce it despite all the causes mentioned above, then use this treatment: Take a pole that is two cubits long and four fingers wide. Its thickness should be tapered at its end towards the tendon like this:"
"And then place lion's cloths on its round head so that the pole does not injure the patient."
"And then place it beside the tendon of the dislocated muscle and extend the hand or arm over the pole so that the pole is bound to the assistant's arms and the end of the hand."
"And then place the arm upon a ladder according to its width and extend the hand to the other side. Suspend the other half of the body on the other side so that the joint may enter quickly."
When the application of remedies has been completed in any manner possible, then place a soft wool wick dipped in a suitable quantity of olive oil; and immediately apply a prepared plaster made from powdered myrrh with incense and albumen of egg over all the muscle, so that it is wrapped around; and then bind the wick with a proper binding; and let the linen be wrapped around the plaster, and the hand of the patient be tied to his neck, and let him be left thus for seven days without removing his hand.
The sick person should abstain from food until the limb is strengthened, because it facilitates recovery.
Then gradually increase food intake after seven or eight days and exercise the limb; if it becomes firm without softening, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity that occurs in it or for another cause, then cautery should be applied there with three triangles, according to what is said earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the binding is removed and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and bandage are repeatedly reduced upon it, but it is still not firm and catches, and he cannot raise that arm above his head, then you should know that the nerves of the arm which are at the head of the muscle have been either cut or stretched or softened; and you should realize that the joint will never be firmly fixed in its place.
For reducing the dislocation of that which has happened you should place the elbow and shoulder blade or the parts behind it; reduction is made with pressure and extension using the hands until it returns to its proper position.
Then apply another bandage there and care for it until it is healed.
And if there is heaviness in the limb during recovery and slowness in the movement of it, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its former nature.
Chapter XXVII
Of the treatment of dislocation of the hip joint.
The joint of the hip is displaced with difficulty and returns with difficulty; that one is displaced in all parts, especially in front of behind, and its displacement is not hidden from you when it appears leaving an empty appearance or leaving a palpable touch depending on how it is displaced, and when you reduce the displaced hip joint into its socket, that is clearly declared to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the leg nor touch itself with that muscle.
You must reduce the dislocation immediately before an abscess forms, because if it comes to form an abscess, reducing it will be difficult and its healing is not guaranteed; and especially when it happens at the back parts, for it is worse than all other kinds of dislocation and causes more pain and often leads to death.
And its reduction, when it is possible to reduce it, is done by extending the arm with two hands and expanding the elbow, and placing the physician's hands on the joint and pushing it back into place with the palms of his hands together, or with the edge of his palm facing inward as it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is at the front parts, then it returns in this way: by bending the arm with his hand, pressing on the joint with the edge of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer strong extension and much force.
And it is necessary that two assistants extend the arm and hold it firmly in place with two hands so that it does not move during extension.
And moreover, let the arm be turned around on all parts, because it is wrapped in doubled long cloth or linen.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with some oil so that this may help to lubricate the joint and facilitate its reduction; and moreover, he should push the joint back into place with a strong impulse until it returns.
And after its reduction, it should be placed on an empanel with sternutory and expectorant properties made of album of wool, and it should be bandaged with a descending compression bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left at rest for several days.
And after it has healed; and then if the joint is formed in its place, you should remove the linen from it and leave it alone.
And if you see that the joint is not strong, then repeat the emplaster and linen, and leave it alone for a few days until it is strengthened; and then remove it.
But if it happens that there is pain around the reduction of the joint and the condition of the limb is in motion, then administer humidification with baths and gentle friction, compression, until it is alleviated; or place on the joint an alleviation bandage, which is a fatty ointment; and then bind it, leaving it alone during the day and night. And then remove that bandage and put him in a bath; and when he gets out, you should rub the joint with gentle friction, and then apply again over it alleviation bandage, which is a warm ointment, once, twice, and three times. And meanwhile keep bathing him until it is well healed.
And if you wish, place over it a wet and scraped cow's hide with its edges stitched together and stretched over it tightly; do this several times, for that is humid and reduces swelling of the condition.
Chapter XXVIII
Of the treatment of dislocation of luxation, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
Sometimes the hand becomes dislocated and its reduction is easy, whereas with other joints it is difficult. However, care must be taken to reduce it quickly when it happens, before the place becomes inflamed or an abscess forms.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the hand joint of the sick person on a table and the physician extends his hand towards him, placing his palm or his fingers on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already been dislocated onto the front of the hand, then the sick person should place the back of his hand on a table with his fingers extended and the reduction performed. But if the dislocation is posterior, then the position of his hand should be placed internally on the table, so that the physician's hand may cover the prominence of the joint.
"And thus if it returns soon, good. But if not, then immediately press with an embankment to drain the abscess and leave that; and do not repeat this, for if you do, neither will it suffer nor can you achieve reduction of the pus over time."
"But in any case cut through the Roman join according to its tortuosity; and do not reveal any cause to the patient, but that your hand is tired and cannot grip anything; however you know that the nerves are pressed or torn, and therefore heat it with a cautery near its exit and then give some reason for this."
"Therefore when the pain returns, then pause over the embankment that we have recommended, and let it be left and pressed for seven days; and afterwards it shall be released and let it alone, and his hand exercised."
"But if the movement of it is obstructed and there occurs in it any gravity, then use that with cold water and friction several times until it is alleviated."
"Chapter XXIX."
"Concerning the treatment of dislocation of these joints."
"Some times these are completely dislocated."
"And when some of these fingers are dislocated at the back of the palm or at the front of it, then seize the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs until it returns."
"And afterwards support the head of the finger, and suspend that above the part where it is dislocated, and let it alone for two days."
"And afterwards suspend that and extend it until it is completely healed within that day."
"And when it is not completely healed, then apply that according to the said narrative, and do not cease to release it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is firm."
"And do likewise if it is dislocated at the front of the hand, and bind that at that articulation and perform with it your first operation until it is healed."
"And do likewise in this manner when it is dislocated at other articulations."
"Chapter XXX."
"Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back."
"When there occurs at some of the vertebrae of the back or neck a complete dislocation, and several vertebrae are removed from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death quickly overtakes the sick person."
"And a sign of this is that the evacuation of the sick person is involuntary and he cannot retain it; and many times some members of him are paralyzed or both arms of him or both legs of him, or one of those; or one vertebra is removed from its place, for many times several are removed and many times the dislocation occurs in four directions."
"Therefore that is already removed from its posterior articulations and is called 'gibbosity'."
"But the treatment of it is that then you should consider if the gibbosity occurs in childhood, for then there is no cure nor is there salvation from all."
"But concerning those which occur through compression or percussion or in similar manner to those, the Ancients have already described the methods of treatment at length, from which the greater part returns with profit; but I have already abbreviated that of which little excuses the multitude of it, due to my proximity to the intention of this exposition, with which I have briefly explained and formed various instruments for demonstrating this and expounding it."
"And only those are cured which occur properly at the back with what I have counted."
"And that the sick person should be placed on his face with his neck bent near to a wall, and be supported from below with soft cushions so that he is not harmed in his feet."
"And place a straight stick horizontally, put it on the ground at the end of the neck around the head of the sick person, and another stick under his feet at the other end of the foot."
"And let one assistant hold the stick, and it should not be fixed in a socket; and another assistant should hold the other according to that form."
"And wrap around the head of the sick person and tie it on tightly with linen and extend the end of the linen to the stick which is above his head, and bind that there; and then stretch the other linen over his hips down to his knees, and place that against his thighs; and then adjust all the linens and bind them onto the other stick which is above his feet."
"And then each assistant should extend the linen to the part of the stick and let not the two sticks and the linens of their places be removed from where they are placed, but nevertheless they should not be fixed in that way as we have said."
"And the physician should place his palms on the vertebra with strength until it returns, or he may place a table there and then adjust his feet on the table until it returns."
"But if it does not return by this method, then take a table whose width or length is about three braccia and hollow out in the wall which we have said is near to the sick person a place into which the end of the table can fit." "And then place the middle of the table on the vertebra, and the physician should place his feet on the other end, and stretch well until the vertebra is compressed and returns to its place."
"And if you wish, open with a twist using the hand; and this is how you should do it: lie down on the ground in front of the head of the sick person between the two sticks whose length is one braccio, and place your feet between the two sticks similarly; and let the distance between each stick be one palm, and already made in each stick is a hole through which the cord passes, and let each be firmly fixed to the ground so that it is not moved."
"And place a round stick on which is the cord, and let it be turned in the two holes of the two sticks."
"And on one end of it is a hole in which the stick is formed, whose length is one palm with what is turned; and the two other sticks have something similar to that."
"And then stretch the linens which you have stretched on the foot of the sick person in the hole which is above his head, and the linens which you have stretched on his buttocks in the cord which is above his feet."
"And then above each cord let there be an assistant who turns the cord with his hand, and the physician should press the vertebra as we have said."
"And then when the vertebra has returned and the place is closed, it is necessary that you should place a poultice with alum of chalk and lint; and then place on the poultice a piece of cloth from a table whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is about as much as the place where the gibbosity occurs."
"And then bind it with linen as we have said, and feed the sick person on soft food until he is healed."
"And if there is any inflammation remaining in the place at the end of healing, it is necessary that medicines which soften and cleanse be administered with application of the table for a long time, as we have recommended; and a lead plate should already be applied to that place."
"And sometimes inflammation occurs in the back of the hipbones due to that dislocation; and it is that bone which is already inclined toward ruin, so that this medicine should not be applied to it, because much harm could come to you from it."
"Chapter 31."
"On treating a dislocation of the hip."
"You should know that the junction of the hip and the junction of the muscle only suffer dislocation alone, not those with small movement and concavity."
The hip joint is dislocated in four ways: either inward or outward, forward or backward.
It is most commonly dislocated inwardly and less frequently anteriorly or posteriorly.
The sign of inward hip dislocation is that when you press the healthy limb against the affected one, it feels longer and more prominent than the healthy one; the patient cannot flex their thigh and has to be guided by another person who takes hold of the dislocated limb to reduce it.
The sign of outward hip dislocation is that its symptoms are opposite those of inward dislocation.
The sign of anterior hip dislocation is that when the patient extends their limb fully, they cannot flex it without pain and cannot walk with the affected leg in front; urine retention and an abdominal mass are also present. The patient walks with a limp and supports themselves on their heel.
The sign of posterior hip dislocation is that the patient cannot extend or flex their limb at the hip; the affected leg appears shorter than the other and the buttock feels flaccid. The main feature is pain in the groin region.
However, reduction of the different types of hip dislocation should be considered if the dislocation is chronic and old, as it may have become fixed in a certain position. In such cases, traction should be applied according to the specific position of the limb, but not all cases require reduction. If reduction is attempted and fails, no further manipulation should be made at that time.
In cases of recent hip dislocation, one must first reduce the joint and then extend the limb at the hip both anteriorly and posteriorly. This should be done gently on both sides as the reduction may be completed by simple traction without further manipulation being necessary.
If reduction is not achieved by simple traction, then a strong and continuous traction should be applied to the limb from below or by elevating it on pillows. The other assistant should hold the patient's hand above their head while another pulls gently on the distal end of the limb. A third assistant may apply gentle counter-traction at the hip joint using a bandage wrapped around the thigh and held firmly in place.
The limb should be extended or traction applied internally from the anterior hip to the greater trochanter or externally from the posterior hip to the lesser trochanter.
All of these reductions should be done in one attempt while the patient is lying on their back with their buttocks just over the edge of the table, so that they are hanging down.
This method of reduction is common to all four types of hip dislocation.
If the hip dislocation is reduced using the method described above, then that is sufficient. However, if it is not reducible by closed methods, then specific treatment for each type of dislocation will be necessary.
For the closed reduction of an anterior hip dislocation, the patient should be placed on their side with the affected limb up. A bandage is wrapped around the thigh and held firmly at the point between the greater trochanter and the head of the femur. Traction is then applied to the leg in a direction from the anterior aspect of the greater trochanter towards the anterior superior iliac spine.
Another assistant should firmly grasp the patient's arms and push their body away from the affected hip while applying strong traction to the leg externally. This may help reduce the dislocation as it allows the femoral head to slip back into its socket.
This method is the easiest and most common way to reduce an anterior hip dislocation.
If the hip dislocation cannot be reduced by closed methods alone, then both of the patient's legs should be elevated simultaneously with gentle traction using two assistants. One assistant holds each leg just above the ankle and pulls gently while the other supports the buttocks from below to keep them elevated.
Each leg should be elevated about 4 digits ( approximately 10 cm) and the affected limb extended an additional 2 digits (approximately 5 cm) more than the other.
The patient should then be suspended by their arms from a traction apparatus or pulley system while lying on their back. The affected limb should be extended with gentle traction until the limb is approximately 24 inches (60 cm) off the ground.
A strong assistant should then grasp the affected leg just above the knee and apply gentle traction while another assistant applies counter-traction to the body by pulling gently on the waist. As this is done, the hip dislocation should reduce spontaneously with a characteristic "clunk" as the femoral head returns to its socket.
For the closed reduction of an posterior hip dislocation, the patient should be placed in the lateral position with the affected side up as previously described. The assistant should then grasp the leg just above the ankle and apply gentle traction while applying counter-traction to the body by pushing the opposite shoulder gently towards the table.
Two vertical posts should be placed on either side of the patient's leg. The posts are connected by a horizontal bar that is positioned over the affected hip and used to apply traction. A padded U-shaped bar can be inserted into this frame to keep the posts from slipping out of place during traction. Wet towels should be wrapped around the post and bar to prevent skin irritation or burns due to friction.
Each assistant should then apply gentle traction to their respective side while the surgeon uses one hand to guide the reduction of the dislocation according to the technique being used.
For hip dislocations that are anterior and inferior (obturator type), gentle traction should be applied to the leg with the patient lying on their back. The surgeon places one hand on the affected greater trochanter and uses the other hand to grasp the thigh just proximal to the patella. Traction is then applied by adducting and extending the leg at the hip while maintaining flexion of the knee.
For posterior hip dislocations, the patient should not be placed in extension as this can cause further displacement of the femoral head. Instead, the patient should be positioned on their back with a pillow or firm support under the affected buttock to elevate the hip slightly. The leg is then positioned in slight abduction and external rotation with the knee flexed, similar to the position used for shoulder dislocations. Gentle traction may be applied if necessary, but this should not cause pain or discomfort to the patient.
Additionally, compression should be applied using a bolster placed between the ilium and ischium on the affected side where the hip joint was located. This can help stabilize the reduced dislocation and prevent redisplacement.
After successful reduction of the hip dislocation, the surgeon should extend both legs and observe for signs of instability. The patient is then turned on their unaffected side while a spica cast is applied to maintain the reduced position and prevent redisplacement. The patient should be kept at bedrest for 3-4 days until follow-up X-rays confirm maintenance of reduction.
After 3-4 days, the spica cast and traction device are removed, and the leg lengths should be checked by comparing the lengths of both legs from the anterior superior iliac spine to the medial malleolus.
Then, if no instability is appreciated on examination under anesthesia, the patient can be allowed to bear weight as tolerated.
However, if any signs of instability are noted during examination under anesthesia, the patient should be re-casted in slight traction for 3-4 days before allowing weight-bearing again.
Approximately, it means: 'And then untie that one, and make them stay in bed for several days until they are recovered.'
'Chapter XXXII'.
'On the treatment of dislocation of the jaw.'
The jaw is dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated towards the outside, towards the inside, or more commonly towards the back. It is not usually dislocated towards the front.
"The sign of dislocation in this case is when you tell the patient to press their knee against something, and then you know that it has been dislocated."
"To restore the dislocation of this knee, you should make the patient sit on their right side so that they can extend their leg if possible; then attach your left hand to the front of their thigh and place your foot between theirs. Quickly bring your right palm behind their kneecap, hold it firmly with both hands, and another servant should extend their foot inward until the kneecap returns to its proper position."
"The sign of reduction of this dislocation is when the knee joint adheres firmly with light effort."
"And afterwards plaster that and make the knee adhere firmly with the joint, and also bandage it tightly with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind it."
"And do not allow the patient to walk around for several days until they are healed and comfortable."
"If reduction of it by you is excused, then administer strong extension with linen strips, which were mentioned in the previous narration of hip joint treatment."
"Chapter XXXIII."
"On the Treatment of Ankle Dislocation."
"The talus sometimes becomes displaced due to slight movement, and sometimes becomes dislocated completely, and this dislocation occurs either on the inner or outer side."
"And the sign of talus dislocation is that often the talus becomes swollen at the site where it has dislocated."
"But the treatment for displacement of the talus is gentle reduction, and this is done by extending it gently with the hands until it returns to its proper place."
"But in the treatment of complete talus dislocation, it is necessary that the patient lies on their back and that they have a strong and reliable assistant standing behind them at the middle point of the dislocation."
"And then place your right hand above the upper part of the foot and your left hand below the foot at the level of the ankle."
"And then traction is applied with the right hand on the foot and with the left hand for two times, after which the left hand extends [the foot], and with the right hand pushes the heel up without turning it."
"And this should be done twice as we have described."
"And then on the third attempt, push the heel up again until you reach the ankle joint."
"So if [the talus] returns in one or two attempts with this method, and if the foot is swollen, that is good; but if not, then repeat the procedure until it returns."
"But if reduction of the talus with this method is prohibited, then have the patient lie on their side on the ground, and use a sturdy stick placed firmly on the ground to wedge between the two sides of the dislocation, and then wrap them up in cloth for fear of frightening the patient."
"And then the assistant holds their part [of the dislocation], and another assistant extends the foot with their hands or with a bandage placed on the neck of the foot; and each assistant extends in turn, while pushing the stick firmly between the two sides of the patient to prevent the body from being pulled towards the side of the extension."
"And then the physician reduces the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy part on the outside to prevent the dislocation from returning."
"And thus when the dislocation returns and it is declared that the reduction has been achieved, then apply plasters and bind [the foot] strongly with bandages, and tie the foot with bandages at the heel."
"And be careful or guard against the nerves which are behind on the talus, so that the bandage is not too tight and does not hurt them severely."
"And then leave him for two or three days; and if the bandage has softened, remove it."
On the third or fourth day, untie him, and forbid the patient from vomiting for 40 days. But if it is forced to go ahead of that space, it cannot be guaranteed that the dislocation has not been dissolved and corrupted, and thus it would not receive treatment.
And if an ulcer forms from the cautery, you should then apply what is described above in our narrative, in another place for treatment and embrocation, until it is removed.
Chapter XXXIV
On the treatment of dislocation of the said hips.
It is necessary that he who has been dislocated be wrapped with little extension without moving him; and it is not difficult, carefully doing so.
But if the dislocation is in some knots of the two thighs, you should then have the patient place his sick leg over something on the ground or on a table, and let him be thus as if he were walking.
And then you stand on the leg, and place your foot over what you have indicated of the joints, and then press that with your foot with strength until it returns, and make sure that it is wrapped and does not appear again in its prominence.
And then place the leg of that side on a table that takes all the leg, which should have two heads, and then stretch it with proper and firm bandages for three days; and then untie it and let him rest from walking for more days until he is strengthened and recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
"Types of dislocation caused by plague or fracture or any injury."
"When such causes occur, if you attempt the cure and restoration of it, often death succeeds."
"And therefore one should not presume about the cure of it unless he is knowledgeable in this art, experienced, merciful and compassionate, cautious, not reckless or presumptuous; and that from the beginning [he uses] medicines that only cauterize wounds, leaving the patient to divine judgment and hoping for salvation from destruction, with alleviation of the illness, where there appears some hope." "And then strive quickly to reduce it at the beginning of the cause before an ulcerous wound forms."
"And then if the limb returns as desired, immediately administer treatment that will prevent ulceration and heal the inflammation with whatever is suitable for it from drying ointments."
"And if dislocation with fracture occurs and the ends of the bone are separated, then you should perform their reduction and do in this what we have said previously about simple dislocations as described above."
"And guard and avoid, according to your ability, that which is within the paths of fear as much as you are able; for this is better for you to owe to your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more helpful to your true body in your time." "So ends the words of Al-Bukhastis perfectly, may God bless them." "Amen."
